dropout-selected candidates from our second catalog.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjabb8ddf6\" href=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/903/2/137/1/apjabb8ddf6_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1637000777&amp;Signature=fJm5qwIwP5im9VUzquMWgIfEBU4%3D\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjabb8ddf6\" href=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/903/2/137/1/apjabb8ddf6_hr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1637000777&amp;Signature=HxdOpGwi%2BlT%2BXnTRQLpAwSbzqw8%3D\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>We note that, as seen in Figures <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjabb8ddf5\">5</a> and <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjabb8ddf6\">6</a>, the objects selected via the dropout technique all have a photometric-redshift distribution that allows for a high-redshift solution, but the best-fit suggests a lower redshift. In that sense we stress that these candidates should be regarded with greater caution.</p><p>A few candidates are predicted by the model to be multiply imaged, such that others from the list may potentially be counter images of the same source. To search for counter images of the high-redshift candidates we lens each candidate image to the source plane and back with the corresponding selection-criteria redshift, and check if other images from the list are within a radius of 3'' from the predicted location of counter images. This practice indicates that objects ID897 and ID311 may be related, but no other obvious counter images are found for other objects. Moreover, note also that this designation is based only on the expected position of multiple images. The magnification ratio, suggested by the model, between ID897 and ID311, is much larger than that observed in practice. In addition, while delensing object ID311 to the source plane and relensing it back to the image plane forms an image close to ID897, no image is predicted near ID311 when relensing-delensing ID897. Thus we conclude also these two objects are not very likely related as well.</p><p>We can compare the high-<em>z</em> yield to the high-redshift number counts in the RELICS survey, where the imaging scheme (filter choice and depth) is similar. Salmon et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib57\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib57\">2020</a>) find 257, 57, and 8 candidate galaxies at <em>z</em><sub>phot</sub>\u00a0\u2243\u00a06, 7, and 8, respectively, over 41 RELICS clusters, so that the average per cluster is 6.3, 1.4, and 0.2 galaxies at these redshifts, respectively, whereas the most prolific RELICS clusters can show above a couple-dozen candidates in total. In that sense, RMJ1212 seems to be comparable to, or somewhat stronger than the average RELICS cluster field in terms of high-<em>z</em> number counts (although note the different selection methods). We emphasize that our list is preliminary, and our candidates, especially the fainter and smaller ones, will need more careful examination when more data is available. For example, the F160W (isophotal) magnitude distribution of the high-<em>z</em> candidates of Salmon et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib57\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib57\">2020</a>) concentrates around AB 27, with relatively few objects around AB 28. Our candidates seem to concentrate close to AB 28, comparable to the nominal depth limit (\u22431<em>\u03c3</em> at AB \u224328.2 for a point source). Note that we only intend to present here these high-z candidate galaxies to facilitate possible follow-up observations before the JWST mission. A more detailed examination of the high-redshift population behind this cluster is warranted, and remains for future work.</p></div><h3 id=\"apjabb8dds4-3\" name=\"apjabb8dds4-3\">4.3.\u00a0Transients</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>Last, we also take advantage of the fact that the cluster was imaged in two different epochs to search for transient sources such as potential supernovae or caustic crossing events. The WFC3/IR integrations through the F105W, F125W, F140W, and F160W wideband filters were acquired first on March 16 2020, UT and at second epoch, nine days later, on March 25, 2020 UT (the ACS WFC imaging was acquired, by contrast, at a single epoch). We have searched the two epochs of WFC3/IR imaging data for variable sources, including supernovae and microlensing events caused as the caustic magnification pattern in the source plane moves relative to the stars in a magnified arc. While microlensing due to a moving caustic pattern will, in general, cause a continuous change in magnification, the characteristic timescale of microlensing peaks (e.g., Kelly et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib33\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib33\">2018</a>; Rodney et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib55\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib55\">2018</a>; Chen et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib16\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib16\">2019</a>; Kaurov et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib32\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib32\">2019</a>) should be approximately two weeks, which was confirmed by that of the Icarus event in MACS J1149 (Kelly et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib33\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib33\">2018</a>). According to the WFC3 Exposure Time Calculator, the 5-<em>\u03c3</em> AB detection limits are approximately 26.0 mag (F105W), 25.9 (F125W), 25.9 (F140W), and 26.0 (F160W), after taking into account the background-noise from the template image. For added sensitivity, we also carried out a search of the coaddition of all WFC3 filters. Visual inspection and SExtractor searches, however, yield no credible (\u22735<em>\u03c3</em>) transients either at the locations of the prominent arcs or at other locations in the imaging footprint. Nevertheless, as the cluster shows several arcs that clearly straddle the critical curves, such as the blue star-forming systems 1\u20133, or systems 4 and c5, it should in principle be useful for future caustic crossing searches.</p></div></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjabb8dds5\" name=\"apjabb8dds5\">5.\u00a0Summary</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>We presented an strong-lensing model for the very rich redMaPPER galaxy cluster RMJ1212 (also known as A1489, RXC J1212.3+2733, or CL1212+2733), in preparation for the WMDF JWST/GTO program (# 1176) that is planned to observed this cluster with NIRCam on JWST. In recent Hubble multiband ACS+WFC3/IR imaging we have identified seven sets of multiple-image sets that were used as constraints, as well as several less secure candidates, and reveal a prominent lens of <em>\u03b8</em><sub>E</sub>\u00a0\u2243\u00a032\u00a0\u00b1\u00a03'' (<em>z</em><sub><em>s</em></sub>\u00a0=\u00a02), and <em>\u03b8</em><sub>E</sub>\u00a0\u2243\u00a039\u00a0\u00b1\u00a04'' (<em>z</em><sub><em>s</em></sub>\u00a0=\u00a010).</p><p>We searched the data for high-redshift candidates. We found four candidate high-redshift objects (<em>z</em>\u00a0\u2273\u00a06) based on a photometric-redshift selection. Applying independently a dropout, Lyman break selection criteria, we uncovered 10 more tentative objects. While we note that our candidates require an additional, future examination, especially when more data become available, these numbers are typical of lensing clusters imaged to similar depths (Salmon et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib57\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib57\">2020</a>). We also searched the data for transient sources. No significant transients were found between the two HST visits (separated by nine days). Nevertheless, there are a few arcs that cross the critical curve and should be useful for caustic crossing searches in future imaging of this cluster, especially with JWST.</p><p>RMJ1212 was provisionally chosen for follow-up based on a relatively large Einstein radius implied from mass-to-light scaling relations in SDSS data. Our analysis here reveals a somewhat (\u223c20%) smaller lens than predicted by the scaling relations, but overall the size and shape of the critical curves are in broad agreement with these blind predictions. Only about half of the multiple-image candidates that we had identified in ground-based data survived the detailed analysis presented here, emphasizing the need for Hubble data for lens modeling and multiple-image identification. In addition, the lensing analysis has revealed that most of the mass is concentrated around the second and third central BCGs, whereas the brightest cluster member seems to be a much smaller concentration of mass than implied by its luminosity. The overall success of our automated procedures to flag RMJ1212 and approximate its lensing properties based solely on the photometry and distribution of cluster members in SDSS data, is another example of a growing ability to map large numbers of cluster lenses automatically in large sky surveys (Zitrin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib73\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib73\">2012</a>). It is not hard to imagine that the combination of such methods (Wong et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib67\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib67\">2012</a>; Stapelberg et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib60\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib60\">2019</a>; Carrasco et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib14\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib14\">2020</a>) including increasingly-popular machine-learning techniques, with wide-field space data as expected from Euclid or the Roman Space Telescope, will enable in a few years time fully automated and increasingly robust lensing analyses of large samples of clusters.</p></div>\n<div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>We kindly thank the reviewer of this work for useful comments. These observations are associated with program ID 15959. class=\"big-branded\">\n\n    <div class=\"publication-name\" id=\"wd-pub-name\">\n\t<div class=\"publication-title\" itemprop=\"name\" itemid=\"periodical\">\n\t\t\t\t<!-- Journal image link tile    <div class=\"partner-logo-alignment\">\n                <!-- Partner logo tile starts -->\n<a href=\"\" class=\"overlay-launch\">\n    <img border=\"0\" src=\"https://cms.iopscience.org/8a2230b8-d09c-11e5-b0b6-759f86a2008e/aas-2018.png?guest=true\" alt=\"The American Astronomical Society, find out more\">\n</a>\n<span class=\"overlay-set\">\n    <div src=\"https://cms.iopscience.org/8a2230b8-d09c-11e5-b0b6-759f86a2008e/aas-2018.png?guest=true\" alt=\"The American Astronomical Society, find out more\">\n            </a>\n        </div>\n        <div class=\"overlay-text\">\n            <p>The American Astronomical Society (AAS), established in 1899 and based in Washington, DC, is the major organization of professional astronomers in North America. Its membership of about 7,000 individuals also includes physicists, mathematicians, geologists, engineers, and others whose research and educational interests lie within the broad spectrum of subjects comprising contemporary astronomy. The mission of the AAS is to enhance and share humanity's scientific understanding of the universe.</p> <p><a href=\"https://aas.org/\">https://aas.org/</a></div>\n        <p class=\"ta-c mt-1 mb-2\"><a href=\"\"></a></p>\n    </div>\n</span>\n<!-- Partner logo tile ends -->\n\n<!-- Partner logo tile starts -->\n<a href=\"\" class=\"overlay-launch\">\n    <img border=\"0\" src=\"https://cms.iopscience.org/7ebd1b32-0439-11e9-b401-cfe9679c40e3/iop-2016.png?guest=true\" alt=\"The Institute of Physics, find out more\">\n</a>\n<span class=\"overlay-set\">\n    <div class=\"tint-screen\"></div>\n    <div class=\"overlay-panel\">\n        <a title=\"Close\"  class=\"close-icon close-overlay\" tabindex=\"0\" role=\"button\" aria-describedby=\"close-icon-description\"><span class=\"icon-close\"></span></a>\n        <p id=\"close-icon-description\" class=\"offscreen-hidden\">Click here to close this overlay, or press the \"Escape\" key on your keyboard.</p>\n        <div class=\"overlay-img\">\n            <a href=\"\">\n                <img border=\"0\" src=\"https://cms.iopscience.org/7ebd1b32-0439-11e9-b401-cfe9679c40e3/iop-2016.png?guest=true\" alt=\"The Institute of Physics, find out more\">\n            </a>\n        </div>\n        <div class=\"overlay-text\">\n            <p>The Institute of Physics (IOP) is a leading scientific society promoting physics and bringing physicists together for the benefit of all. It has a worldwide membership of around 50 000 comprising physicists from all sectors, as well as those with an interest in physics. It works to advance physics research, application and education; and engages with policy makers and the public to develop awareness and understanding of physics. Its publishing company, IOP Publishing, is a world leader in professional scientific communications.</p> <p><a href=\"https://www.iop.org\">https://www.iop.org</a></p></div>\n        <p class=\"ta-c <span class=\"icon-newtab\">\n            </a></p>\n        </div>\n\n    <!-- End Email Alert -->\n<!-- End Email Alert --><!-- Start Citation Alert Link -->\n                        <p>\n<div class=\"jnl-notifications-wrapper\">\n    <a class=\"wd-jnl-email-alert\" href=\"https://myiopscience.iop.org/signin?origin=a0&return=https%3A%2F%2Fiopscience.iop.org%2Fmyiopscience%2Falerts%2Fsubscribe%3FarticleID%3D0004-637X%2F903%2F2%2F137\"><span\n            class=\"icon-alarm icon-alarm--jhp\"></span>Create citation alert</a>\n</div><!-- End Citation Alert Link -->\n                    </div>\n\t\t</div>\n    </div>\n    <!-- Article information ends -->\n<!-- Start Info top-->\n    <!-- end Info top --><div class=\"article-content\">\n\t<span id=\"articleId\" class=\"hide\">0004-637X/903/2/137</span>\n<!-- Start Abstract -->\n    <h2 id=\"artAbst\" class=\"collapse-blocked\">Abstract</h2>\n        <!-- Start article video abstract -->\n\t<!-- End article video abstract -->\n\t<div class=\"article-text wd-jnl-art-abstract cf\" itemprop=\"description\">\n            <p>We present a first strong-lensing model for the galaxy cluster RM J121218.5+273255.1 (<i>z</i>\u00a0=\u00a00.35; hereafter RMJ1212; also known as A1489). This cluster is among the top 0.1% richest clusters in the redMaPPer catalog; it is significantly detected in X-ray and through the Sunyaev\u2013Zel'dovich effect in ROSAT and Planck data, respectively, and its optical luminosity distribution implies a very large lens, following mass-to-light scaling relations. Based on these properties it was chosen for the Webb Medium Deep Fields (WMDF) James Webb Space Telescope/Guaranteed Time Observations program. In preparation for this program, RMJ1212 was recently imaged with Gemini Multi-Object Spectrograph (GMOS) on Gemini North and in seven optical and near-infrared bands with the Hubble Space Telescope (HST). We use these data to map the inner mass distribution of the cluster, uncovering various sets of multiple images. We also search for high-redshift candidates in the data, as well as for transient sources. We find over a dozen high-redshift (<i>z</i>\u00a0\u2273\u00a06) candidates based on both photometric redshift and the dropout technique. No prominent (\u22735<i>\u03c3</i>) transients were found in the data between the two HST visits. Our lensing analysis reveals a relatively large lens with an effective Einstein radius of <i>\u03b8</i><sub>E</sub>\u00a0\u2243\u00a032\u00a0\u00b1\u00a03'' (<i>z</i><sub><i>s</i></sub>\u00a0=\u00a02), in broad agreement with the scaling-relation expectations. RMJ1212 demonstrates that powerful lensing clusters can be selected in a robust and automated way following the light-traces-mass assumption.</p></div>\n    <!-- End abstract -->\n<!-- Start Export citation and abstract tools -->\n\t<p>\n\t\t<small>Export citation and abstract</small>\n\t\t<span class=\"btn-multi-block\">\n\t\t\t<a bottom -->\n<!-- Start article full text -->\n\t<div xmlns:book=\"http://api.iop.org/Book/1.0/\" itemprop=\"articleBody\" class=\"wd-jnl-art-full-text article-text\">\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjabb8dds1\" name=\"apjabb8dds1\">1.\u00a0Introduction</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>Strong lensing by galaxy clusters has both enabled studies of the dark matter distribution in cluster cores (Bartelmann <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib5\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib5\">2010</a>; Kneib &amp; Natarajan <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib35\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib35\">2011</a>, for reviews), and a magnified view of high-redshift galaxies, often not accessible otherwise (e.g., Franx et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib25\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib25\">1997</a>; Frye &amp; Broadhurst <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib26\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib26\">1998</a>; Ellis et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib23\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib23\">2001</a>; Bradley et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib11\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib11\">2008</a>; Zheng et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib71\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib71\">2012</a>; Coe et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib19\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib19\">2013</a>; Hashimoto et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib29\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib29\">2018</a>).</p><p>Deep cluster-lensing campaigns with Hubble, such as the Cluster Lensing and Supernova with Hubble (CLASH; Postman et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib51\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib51\">2012</a>) and the Reionization Lensing Cluster Survey (RELICS; Coe et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib18\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib18\">2019</a>), have supplied hundreds of high-redshift (z\u00a0\u2273\u00a06) galaxies in the heart of the reionization era (Bradley et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib12\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib12\">2014</a>; Salmon et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib57\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib57\">2020</a>). The Hubble Frontier Fields (HFF; Lotz et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib39\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib39\">2017</a>) cluster-lensing survey targeted six of the most prominent lensing clusters known (with Einstein radii of \u223c25''\u201355''\u2014for <em>z</em><sub><em>s</em></sub>\u00a0\u2243\u00a02 sources) in order to maximize the high-redshift science return. The HFF has delivered some of the highest redshift galaxies known to date (e.g., Laporte et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib37\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib37\">2017</a>) and a large sample of the faintest high-redshift galaxies in the reionization era (e.g., Zheng et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib72\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib72\">2014</a>; McLeod et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib43\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib43\">2015</a>) reaching as intrinsically faint as <em>M</em><sub>UV</sub>\u00a0\u2243\u00a0\u221216 at <em>z</em>\u00a0\u2243\u00a08 (e.g., Atek et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib3\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib3\">2015</a>; Bouwens et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib10\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib10\">2017</a>; Livermore et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib38\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib38\">2017</a>; Ishigaki et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib31\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib31\">2018</a>; Yue et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib70\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib70\">2018</a>). This is of particular importance because it is believed that faint galaxies are responsible for the reionization of the universe (e.g., Yan &amp; Windhorst <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib68\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib68\">2004</a>; Robertson et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib54\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib54\">2015</a>; Finkelstein <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib24\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib24\">2016</a>; Bouwens et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib10\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib10\">2017</a>). Remarkably, the HFF has not only boosted high-redshift science, but it also led to serendipitous discoveries such as the first resolved multiply-imaged supernova (Kelly et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib34\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib34\">2015</a>) and first cosmological caustic crossing events of high-redshift stars (Kelly et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib33\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib33\">2018</a>; Rodney et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib55\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib55\">2018</a>; Chen et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib16\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib16\">2019</a>; Kaurov et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib32\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib32\">2019</a>).</p><p>The next leap in these scientific fields is anticipated to take place with the James Webb Space Telescope (JWST). The JWST will enable an extended wavelength coverage and a much deeper view of lensed galaxies (e.g., Mason et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib42\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib42\">2015</a>), pushing toward intrinsically fainter and higher redshift galaxies\u2014one of its primary goals. JWST will also reveal many more transient phenomena and caustic crossings in lensing clusters (Venumadhav et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib65\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib65\">2017</a>; Oguri et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib47\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib47\">2018</a>; Windhorst et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib66\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib66\">2018</a>), enabling important constraints on the composition of dark matter (e.g., Diego et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib21\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib21\">2018</a>; Dai et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib20\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib20\">2018</a>). As such, to maximize these science cases\u2014that of high-redshift and reionization in particular\u2014a number of JWST Guaranteed Time Observations (GTO) programs have chosen to concentrate on prominent cluster lenses.</p><p>Most of the prominent cluster lenses known to date were chosen based on their gas properties, for example, in X-rays\u2014such as clusters from the MAssive Cluster Survey (MACS; e.g., Ebeling et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib22\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib22\">2010</a>), or the Sunyaev\u2013Zel'dovich (SZ) effect\u2014such as the Planck clusters (e.g., Planck Collaboration et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib50\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib50\">2016</a>) imaged in the RELICS program. However, there appear to be many more massive optically-selected rich clusters in the sky that are not necessarily bright enough in X-ray or SZ to be included in these samples, but their projected matter distribution is concentrated enough to form a large strong lens (see, e.g., Wong et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib67\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib67\">2012</a>). Such cases (see also Umetsu <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib63\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib63\">2020</a> Section 6.2) include the famous A370 HFF cluster with <em>\u03b8</em><sub>E</sub>\u00a0\u2243\u00a040'' (Richard et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib53\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib53\">2010</a>); CL0024+1654 with <em>\u03b8</em><sub>E</sub>\u00a0\u2243\u00a035'' (Zitrin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib74\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib74\">2009</a>); or PLCK G165.7+67.0, which despite its naming was not in fact chosen based on its SZ signal, yet shows an impressive abundance of lensed features (Frye et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib27\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib27\">2019</a>).</p><p>The JWST Medium Deep Fields GTO program (WMDF; PI: Windhorst) has chosen to capitalize on such cases and, in addition to various gas-selected clusters that are well-known prominent lenses, set to observe a few other clusters chosen based on a mix of different probes, including the rich, redMaPPer galaxy cluster RM J121218.5+273255.1 (<em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a00.35; RMJ1212 hereafter; also known as A1489, RXC J1212.3+2733, or CL1212+2733), as identified in the Sloan Digital Sky Survey (SDSS; York et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib69\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib69\">2000</a>) data. RMJ1212 was provisionally chosen for WMDF mainly as an optically-selected strong lens based on the following properties: it is among the 0.1% richest clusters in the redMaPPer catalog (Rykoff et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib56\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib56\">2014</a>, <em>\u03bb</em>\u00a0=\u00a0158.24\u00a0\u00b1\u00a06.03 in the public redMaPPer sdss dr8 v6.3 catalog,<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjabb8ddfn1\">18</a></sup>\n ranking 33rd out of over \u223c26,000 clusters); it has a high ecliptic latitude (<em>b</em> \u2273 30\u00b0) minimizing zodiacal near-infrared background light (an important consideration for JWST high-redshift targets); it had a prominent lensing strength, and large Einstein radius of \u223c40'' \u00b1 20% predicted from mass-to-light rescaling of SDSS clusters (Zitrin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib73\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib73\">2012</a>); its preliminary velocity dispersion estimated from few measured cluster members in SDSS is high, over \u223c900 km/s; and given that it is significantly detected in X-ray (e.g., 6.13<em>\u03c3</em> in ROSAT; or an estimated mass of <em>M</em><sub>500</sub> = 10.3 \u00b1 2.1 <b>&#x00d7;</b> 10<sup>14</sup> <em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub>, Mantz et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib40\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib40\">2010</a>), and SZ (10.09<em>\u03c3</em> in Planck; an SZ mass proxy of <em>M</em><sub>500</sub> = 7.50 \u00b1 0.44 <b>&#x00d7;</b> 10<sup>14</sup> <em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub>,<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjabb8ddfn2\">19</a></sup>\n Planck Collaboration et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib50\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib50\">2016</a>).</p><p>Following these promising properties, we targeted RMJ1212 with Gemini Multi-Object Spectrograph (GMOS) on Gemini-N in imaging mode and detected various sets of potentially multiply-imaged galaxies (see Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjabb8ddf1\">1</a>). Since space data is typically required to verify the tentative identification of multiple images, RMJ1212 was also recently imaged with the Hubble Space Telescope (HST) in seven bands (see Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjabb8ddf2\">2</a> and Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjabb8dds2\">2</a> for details). Here, we present a first strong-lensing analysis of the cluster in these data. We also search these data for bright, high-redshift dropout galaxies lensed by the cluster and for transient events appearing between the two HST visits of the cluster. Updated lens models, including with spectroscopic redshifts for the multiple images,<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjabb8ddfn3\">20</a></sup>\n and extending out to the weak-lensing regime, are planned for future work.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjabb8ddf1\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjabb8ddf1\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/903/2/137/1/apjabb8ddf1_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1637000777&amp;Signature=JOvQZKq%2BamAdfT6DcXGB2KgTK1o%3D\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 1.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 1.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/903/2/137/1/apjabb8ddf1_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1637000777&amp;Signature=JOvQZKq%2BamAdfT6DcXGB2KgTK1o%3D\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 1.</strong>\u00a0Preliminary lensing properties of RMJ1212 from ground-based data. Their location follows nicely the critical curves predicted, suggesting that indeed RMJ1212 is a prominent lens, but HST observations were required to corroborate this, secure these identifications, and construct a credible lens model for the cluster, which are our goals here.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjabb8ddf1\" href=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/903/2/137/1/apjabb8ddf1_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1637000777&amp;Signature=JOvQZKq%2BamAdfT6DcXGB2KgTK1o%3D\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjabb8ddf1\" href=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/903/2/137/1/apjabb8ddf1_hr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1637000777&amp;Signature=xBkk07TyCTq54fcmbx2xYjnDk3s%3D\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjabb8ddf2\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjabb8ddf2\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/903/2/137/1/apjabb8ddf2_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1637000777&amp;Signature=mh3%2FdgOMw58cSaZJsu95ggqcRp0%3D\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 2.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; Shown is an RGB color-composite image from the 7-band HST data. Multiple-image sets are marked on the image and the critical curves from our model are overlaid in light blue for a source at a redshift similar to that of the main arc (system 1; <em>z</em><sub>phot</sub>\u00a0\u223c\u00a02.7), and in red for a source redshift of <em>z</em><sub><em>s</em></sub>\u00a0\u2243\u00a010. Bright cluster members, some of whose parameters were individually optimized in the minimization (see text), are noted with A, B, C, and D.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjabb8ddf2\" href=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/903/2/137/1/apjabb8ddf2_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1637000777&amp;Signature=mh3%2FdgOMw58cSaZJsu95ggqcRp0%3D\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjabb8ddf2\" href=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/903/2/137/1/apjabb8ddf2_hr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1637000777&amp;Signature=it1ES5euLm7op%2FP3%2Fbj4V9xt0lc%3D\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>The paper is organized as follows: in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjabb8dds2\">2</a> we detail the observations of the cluster and their data reduction. Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjabb8dds3\">3</a> outlines the light-traces-mass (LTM) lens modeling code, and its implementation to RMJ1212. The modeling results are presented and discussed in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjabb8dds4\">4</a>, and concluded in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjabb8dds5\">5</a>. Throughout this work we use a \u039bCDM cosmology with \u03a9<sub><em>M</em></sub>\u00a0=\u00a00.3, \u03a9<sub><em>M</em></sub>\u00a0=\u00a00.7, and <em>H</em><sub>0</sub>\u00a0=\u00a070 km s<sup>\u22121</sup> Mpc<sup>\u22121</sup>. Unless otherwise stated, we generally use AB magnitudes (Oke &amp; Gunn <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib48\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib48\">1983</a>), and errors are 1<em>\u03c3</em>.</p></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjabb8dds2\" name=\"apjabb8dds2\">2.\u00a0Observations and Data Reduction</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>The galaxy cluster RMJ1212 was imaged in queue mode with GMOS on Gemini-N (program ID: GN-2019A-Q-903, PI: Zitrin) on 2019 March 12, in the <em>g</em> (8\u00a0<b>&#x00d7;</b>\u00a0600 s) and <em>r</em> (\u22437.5\u00a0<b>&#x00d7;</b>\u00a0600 s) bands, and on 2019 April 4, in the <em>i</em> (8\u00a0<b>&#x00d7;</b>\u00a0300 s) band. Data were retrieved from the Gemini archive and reduced using standard procedures with the Gemini I<span class=\"small-caps\">raf</span> pipeline. Astrometry, based on SDSS, was obtained using S<span class=\"small-caps\">camp</span> (Bertin <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib9\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib9\">2006</a>). The images, after background subtraction, were coadded using S<span class=\"small-caps\">warp</span> (Bertin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib8\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib8\">2002</a>), and zero-points for the <em>g,r,i</em> stacks were obtained via a comparison with SDSS. A color-composite image of these data is shown in the right panel of Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjabb8ddf1\">1</a>.</p><p>In preparation for the WMDF JWST/GTO program, and in order to construct a detailed lens model, identify high-redshift candidates, and form baseline observations for future transient detection, RMJ1212 was recently observed with Hubble for five orbits (program ID: 15959, PI: Zitrin). The cluster was observed for a total of about 1 orbit in each of the F435W (1934s), F606W (1904s), and F814W (1934s) filters with the Advanced Camera for Surveys (ACS) Wide Field Channel (WFC); and for a total of about half an orbit in each of the F105W (1212s), F125W (912s), F140W (912s), and F160W (1612s) filters with the Wide-Field Camera 3\u2014infrared channel (WFC3/IR). One F140W exposure suffered from a guiding problem. To fix this, the readout (\u223c50 s) in which the drift was detected was discarded, and not used in constructing the final images. Observations were divided into two visits, both to relax scheduling constraints, and to allow\u2014albeit at low probability\u2014for transient searches, and were carried out on 2020 March 16 and 2020 March 25.</p><p>Mosaic images for all the HST exposures were produced from the calibrated exposures by running the MosaicDrizzle pipeline (Koekemoer et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib36\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib36\">2011</a>), specialized for this proposal and updated to use the latest drizzlepac routines, achieving milliarcsecond-level astrometric alignment across all the different filters for ACS/WFC and WFC3/IR. Two sets of mosaics were produced, at scales of 0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />03 and 0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />06 per pixel, with all the pixels aligned to the same astrometric grid, with North up, and registered onto the Gaia DR2 reference frame. The 0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />03 mosaics are most useful for studying fine morphological details, especially since they sub-sample the native ACS pixel scale, while the 0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />06 mosaics are more generally useful for producing catalogs across both WFC3/IR and ACS, which we describe in this section.</p><p>Throughout our paper we work with several photometric catalogs. First, for the lens model, we need a list of potential cluster members and their photometry. We run SE<span class=\"small-caps\">xtractor</span> (Bertin &amp; Arnouts <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib7\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib7\">1996</a>) version 2.25.0 on the F814W band, and then in dual mode on all other HST images with the F814W as the detection image. Cluster members are identified following the red-sequence in a color\u2013magnitude diagram, where we use the F606W-F814W color versus the F814W magnitude. Members are chosen within \u00b10.15 mag from this sequence (defined as (mag<sub>F606W</sub>\u00a0\u2212\u00a0mag<sub>F814W</sub>)\u00a0=\u00a00.05 \u2217 mag<sub>F814W</sub>+0.29), and up to a magnitude of mag<sub>F814W</sub>\u00a0=\u00a022.5. The list of cluster members is then examined visually, and updated as needed. We also include an apparently foreground, <em>z</em><sub>phot</sub>\u00a0\u2243\u00a00.2, bright galaxy [at R.A.\u00a0= 12:12:17.316, decl.\u00a0=\u00a0+27:33:31.81] near the cluster center, but with a 1/20 weight, i.e., a fraction of the mass implied by its flux. This photometric catalog is not used for any other purpose.</p><p>We also produced a set of catalogs using the RELICS pipeline (Coe et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib18\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib18\">2019</a>), which generates combined RGB images from various bands, and runs both SExtractor (version 2.8.6) and the Bayesian Photometric Redshift code (BPZ; Ben\u00edtez et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib6\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib6\">2004</a>; Coe et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib17\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib17\">2006</a>). Photometry is corrected for Galactic extinction using the IR dust emission maps of Schlafly &amp; Finkbeiner (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib58\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib58\">2011</a>). Note that with seven bands spanning a wider wavelength range and more accurately calibrated data than the ground-based data, only the HST data are used for fitting photometric redshifts. The RELICS pipeline creates two source catalogs: \"acs-wfc3ir,\" based on detections in a weighted stack of all HST images (ACS+WFC3/IR), optimized to detect most objects; and \"wfc3ir,\" based on detections in a weighted stack of the WFC3/IR images, and using a finer background grid and more aggressive deblending, optimized to detect smaller high-redshift galaxies. Independently, we also build another alternative catalog based on HST photometry to search for faint high-redshift galaxies. We run SExtractor in dual mode the seven HST bands, with a similarly weighted stack of all WFC3/IR images as the detection image. We use a local background estimate with <em>back</em>_<em>size</em>\u00a0=\u00a016, <em>detect</em>_<em>minarea</em>\u00a0=\u00a08, <em>detect</em>_<em>thresh</em>\u00a0=\u00a01, <em>analysis</em>_<em>thresh</em>\u00a0=\u00a01.5 and <em>deblend</em>_<em>nthresh</em>\u00a0=\u00a016 to improve source detection. Note that all magnitudes are measured and given hereafter in isophotal apertures, and corrected for galactic extinction.</p><p>We also show here a smoothed X-ray map taken with Chandra on 2003 January 11 (Obs. ID 5767, PI: Vikhlinin), with an exposure time of 15.0 ks. We use the high-resolution ACIS Primary data product, smoothed with a 20-pixel Gaussian. These X-ray data are only used qualitatively to show the X-ray centroid (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjabb8ddf3\">3</a>).</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjabb8ddf3\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjabb8ddf3\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/903/2/137/1/apjabb8ddf3_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1637000777&amp;Signature=oI5TnlZ2dImjRb7WUYx7%2BJtHwdY%3D\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 3.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 3.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/903/2/137/1/apjabb8ddf3_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1637000777&amp;Signature=oI5TnlZ2dImjRb7WUYx7%2BJtHwdY%3D\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 3.</strong>\u00a0Projected mass density of RMJ1212. We show <em>\u03ba</em>, the surface-mass density distribution in units of the critical density for strong lensing, for the source redshift adopted for the main arc (<em>z</em><sub><em>s</em></sub>\u00a0=\u00a02.7; systems 1, 2, 3). Overlaid black contours follow a smoothed X-ray map from Chandra. Capital letters A, B, C, and D mark the four central brightest cluster galaxies as in Figure <a href=\"#apjabb8ddf2\">2</a>. From the lensing analysis (and its resulting <em>\u03ba</em> map shown) it becomes apparent that the main mass concentration in centered on galaxy B, in agreement with the X-ray contours, and despite galaxy A being comparable in optical brightness (and in fact slightly brighter, see Section <a class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjabb8dds4\">4</a>). The prominent contours on the right-hand side correspond to a point source in the X-ray data, coinciding with a prominent spiral galaxy (RA\u00a0=\u00a012:12:12.829, DEC\u00a0=\u00a0+27:33:12.831) and likely suggesting the presence of an active galactic nucleus. X-ray data were interpolated for clarity and should be regarded as qualitative only.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjabb8ddf3\" href=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/903/2/137/1/apjabb8ddf3_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1637000777&amp;Signature=oI5TnlZ2dImjRb7WUYx7%2BJtHwdY%3D\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjabb8ddf3\" href=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/903/2/137/1/apjabb8ddf3_hr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1637000777&amp;Signature=tRDcw3Cak%2Bs9vAk67O92JU3OHzo%3D\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>The reduced HST images, combined color images, and catalogs, are made publicly available online.<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjabb8ddfn4\">21</a></sup>\n The lens model now detailed is also available in the same library.</p></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjabb8dds3\" name=\"apjabb8dds3\">3.\u00a0Strong-lensing Modeling of RMJ1212</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>We use here the LTM lens modeling code of Zitrin et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib74\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib74\">2009</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib75\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib75\">2015</a>, and references therein), which is especially useful for the analysis of new lenses as it is inherently capable of guiding the detection of multiple-image sets (Carrasco et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib14\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib14\">2020</a>). More complete details of the formalism can be found in the above references, and we give here only a broad outline.</p><p>The deflection field is modeled as a sum of three components. The first component maps the projected mass density distribution of the cluster galaxies, each modeled with a simple power-law (<em>q</em>) surface-mass density, scaled with the galaxy's luminosity and normalized to a desired lensing distance, or redshift, by some factor (<em>K</em>). The second component is the dark matter map which is obtained by smoothing the galaxy map with a Gaussian kernel of width <em>S</em>. The two components are then added with a relative weight <em>k</em><sub><em>gal</em></sub>, reflecting the ratio of luminous to dark matter. The third component, contributing only to the deflection field, but not to the mass density, is an external shear of strength <em>\u03b3</em><sub><em>ex</em></sub> and position angle <em>\u03d5</em><sub><em>ex</em></sub>, which allows for greater effective elongation of the critical curves and accounts for the contribution of larger-scale structure. The model thus comprises six main parameters: <em>q</em>, <em>S</em>, <em>K</em>, <em>k</em><sub><em>gal</em></sub>, <em>\u03b3</em><sub><em>ex</em></sub>, and <em>\u03d5</em><sub><em>ex</em></sub>.</p><p>We often introduce ellipticites and position angles as well as central cores for a few key cluster members, such as the brightest (and thus most massive) galaxies. These can either be set as fixed, or be minimized as well (adding, correspondingly, to the number of free parameters). In addition, it is often beneficial to leave the relative weight (i.e., the relative mass-to-light ratio) of some key galaxies free as well. Similarly, the lensing distance (i.e., essentially, redshift) of systems with poorly constrained redshifts can also be left to be freely optimized.</p><p>The optimization of the model is carried out by minimizing, using a <em>\u03c7</em><sup>2</sup> function, the distance between multiple images and their positions predicted by the model. This is done via a Monte-Carlo Markov Chain with a Metropolis-Hastings algorithm (e.g., Hastings <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib30\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib30\">1970</a>). We also include some annealing in the procedure, and the chain typically runs for several thousand steps after the burn-in stage. Errors are calculated from the same Markov chain.</p><p>For modeling RMJ1212 we start with all galaxies fixed, and construct a simple model minimized by two sets of obvious multiple images: images 1.1+1.2, and images 4.1+4.2, fixing them to their best-fit photometric redshifts (Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjabb8ddt1\">1</a>). With this preliminary model we iteratively predict the location of counter images and find additional systems by sending notable arclets across the field to the source plane and back, probing a range of redshifts. In total, we find 31 likely multiple images and candidates of about 8\u201310 background sources (systems 1, 2, and 3 may correspond to a single background object). With these, we construct the final model presented here. To anchor the model we fix the redshift of all systems to roughly their average, or typical, best BPZ value, listed in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjabb8ddt1\">1</a> as well (although some minor differences may exist due to updates to the catalog; these only weakly affect the derived <em>D</em><sub><em>ls</em></sub>/<em>D</em><sub><em>s</em></sub> ratio). We leave the weight of the four central brightest cluster galaxies (BCGs A, B, C, D in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjabb8ddf2\">2</a>) free, and allow for ellipticity for the brightest two (A and B). For the southern BCG around which system 6 appears, BCG \"B,\" we allow the ellipticity to vary around its values measured from SExtractor. No cores are incorporated for cluster galaxies, aside from this southern one, for which we allow a small core. In total, the model includes 13 free parameters, optimized using uniform flat priors. Image position uncertainties were adopted to be 0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />5, whereas for systems 1, 3, and 6, we adopted 0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />1 (to give them more weight in the modeling).</p><div tabindex=\"-1\" id=\"apjabb8ddt1\" class=\"boxout colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">\"</td>\n</tr>\n<tr valign=\"top\">\n<td class=\"rowsep\" colspan=\"5\" rowspan=\"1\">\n\n</td>\n</tr>\n<tr valign=\"top\">\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"left\">c10.1</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">12:12:16.586</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">+27:33:36.49</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">2.578 [0.543\u20132.738]</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">\u22ef</td>\n</tr>\n<tr valign=\"top\">\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"left\">c10.2</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">12:12:16.153</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">+27:33:29.88</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">0.928 [0.600\u20132.394]</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">\u22ef</td>\n</tr>\n<tr valign=\"top\">\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"left\">c10.3</td>\n<td [0.115\u20133.056]</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">\u22ef</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody></table><p><small>\n<p><strong>Note.</strong>\u00a0Multiple images and candidates. <em>C</em>olumn\u00a01:\u00a0ID; Columns\u00a02 &amp; 3<em>:</em>\u00a0R.A. and decl., in J2000.0; Column\u00a04:\u00a0best photometric redshift from BPZ, and its 95% confidence interval; Column\u00a05:\u00a0the redshift of the system as adopted for the modeling. \"c\" stands for candidate image, whose identification was less secure and it was not used in the minimization.</p>\n</small></p><p class=\"franklin fs-16\">\n\t\t\tDownload table as:\u00a0\n            <span class=\"btn-multi-block\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-ascii\" href=\"/0004-637X/903/2/137/suppdata/apjabb8ddt1_ascii.txt?doi=10.3847/1538-4357/abb8dd\" target=\"_blank\">ASCII</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-typeset typeset-table-img\" href=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/903/2/137/1/apjabb8ddt1_lr.gif?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1637000777&amp;Signature=tI77ZuEbkmCh9Dho%2BaLYsiDhCZw%3D\" target=\"_blank\">Typeset image</a></span></p></div></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjabb8dds4\" name=\"apjabb8dds4\">4.\u00a0Results and Discussion</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><h3 id=\"apjabb8dds4-1\" name=\"apjabb8dds4-1\">4.1.\u00a0Lens Modeling</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>The reproduction of multiple images is excellent, and a few examples are given in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjabb8ddf4\">4</a>. Quantitatively, the resulting best-fit model has an rms of 1<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />44 in reproducing the position of multiple images. Such values are typical for LTM models of moderately complex, large lenses, although they are larger than those typically found for parametric models, in part due to the LTM's finite grid resolution. In addition, the resulting rms is also larger than the 0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />5 positional uncertainty adopted here for most images. In that sense it should be noted that the range of accepted models and thus the deduced uncertainties are coupled to the adopted value.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjabb8ddf4\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjabb8ddf4\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/903/2/137/1/apjabb8ddf4_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1637000777&amp;Signature=Q7OOuSSA1%2BX%2FBswa%2BZCZKZpRIWw%3D\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 4.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 4.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.68.1/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/903/2/137/1/apjabb8ddf4_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1637000777&amp;Signature=Q7OOuSSA1%2BX%2FBswa%2BZCZKZpRIWw%3D\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 4.</strong>\u00a0Reproduction of multiple images by our model. We show the reproduction of system 1 (and 2 and 3), by lensing the left side of the arc, image 1.1 (also 2.1 and 3.1), to the source plane and back through the lens; system 4, by lensing image 4.1 to the source plane and back; system 6, by lensing image 6.1 to the source plane and back; system 8, by lensing image 8.3 to the source plane and back; and system 9, by lensing image 9.1 to the source plane and back. For each system the upper row shows the images in the data and the bottom row the reproduction by the model. Although some minor differences exist, the prediction of the model evidently reproduces the observed images well, strengthening their identification.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjabb8ddf4\" href=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/903/2/137/1/apjabb8ddf4_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1637000777&amp;Signature=Q7OOuSSA1%2BX%2FBswa%2BZCZKZpRIWw%3D\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjabb8ddf4\" href=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/903/2/137/1/apjabb8ddf4_hr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1637000777&amp;Signature=sUPmmWNUOEwnd1SZ2BlZXYf3zjw%3D\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>The projected mass density map of the best-fit model is shown in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjabb8ddf3\">3</a>. An interesting point to note is that while galaxy A in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjabb8ddf2\">2</a> is slightly brighter (mag<sub>F814W</sub>\u00a0=\u00a017.37 AB) than galaxies B (mag<sub>F814W</sub>\u00a0=\u00a017.68 AB) and C (mag<sub>F814W</sub>\u00a0=\u00a017.93 AB), the latter two seem to represent locations of much greater concentrations of mass, with galaxy B being apparently in the center of the potential well of the cluster. This finding agrees very well also with the X-ray signal, which is concentrated on galaxy B (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjabb8ddf3\">3</a>, black contours). In addition, we also make use of the notable, <em>z</em><sub>phot</sub>\u00a0\u223c\u00a00.9 arc just below galaxy A (see Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjabb8ddf2\">2</a>) to verify that indeed galaxy A's weight agrees with its shape. We find that with a redshift of <em>z</em>\u00a0\u223c\u00a01 the arc is reproduced very well, supporting the weight of galaxy A. While this arc does not appear to be multiply imaged, we also acknowledge the possibility that the western, fuzzy part of the arc may be locally, multiply imaged (by galaxy A, mainly).</p><p>The corresponding critical curves of the best-fit model are overlaid on an image of the cluster in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjabb8ddf2\">2</a>, where multiple images are marked as well. The effective Einstein radius we find is relatively large, 31.6\u00a0\u00b1\u00a03<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />2 for a source at <em>z</em><sub><em>s</em></sub>\u00a0=\u00a02, where the effective Einstein radius is defined as the radius of the area enclosed within the critical curves if it were a circle. The mass in that critical area is 1.53\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.31\u00a0<b>&#x00d7;</b>\u00a010<sup>14</sup> <em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub>. The quoted errors on the Einstein radius and mass are nominal values (taken as 10% and 20%, respectively), reflecting typical errors seen between different models (e.g., Zitrin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib75\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib75\">2015</a>); the statistical uncertainties are somewhat smaller. For the assigned redshift of systems 1\u20133, <em>z</em><sub><em>s</em></sub>\u00a0=\u00a02.7, the light-blue curves shown in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjabb8ddf2\">2</a> have an effective <em>\u03b8</em><sub>E</sub>\u00a0\u2243\u00a034'', and for a source at <em>z</em><sub><em>s</em></sub>\u00a0=\u00a010 (red curves therein), they reach \u224339''. These estimates will be revised once multiple-image redshifts become available, and perhaps when more multiple images, especially around the northern end, are identified.</p><p>It has been known that substantial substructure projected near the core in merging clusters, as seen in RMJ1212, boosts the Einstein radius (Torri et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib62\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib62\">2004</a>; Redlich et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib52\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib52\">2012</a>). Searching for the largest and most efficient lenses is important for a variety of studies. Given the shape of the cosmological mass function and the hierarchical mass build up, massive clusters are rarer, and their numbers have direct implications for structure formation and evolution models, as well as for cosmological models. While a total mass does not guarantee a large lens, overall the Einstein radius should increase with the mass of the cluster (albeit with a large scatter), and predictions can be made for the universal Einstein radius distribution based on an input mass function, cosmology, and assumptions on the shape of the clusters (e.g., Oguri &amp; Blandford <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib46\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib46\">2009</a>). In addition, and especially as we prepare for the next generation space telescope, JWST, a primary goal of which is detecting the first galaxies, we wish to find those lenses that maximize the high-redshift galaxy yield. Merging galaxy clusters, especially those with elongated shapes, are known to have a boosted lensing cross section (e.g., Meneghetti et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib44\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib44\">2003</a>; Zitrin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib76\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib76\">2013</a>; Acebron et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib1\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib1\">2019</a>), such as the HFF clusters (Lotz et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib39\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib39\">2017</a>; Vega-Ferrero et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib64\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib64\">2019</a>), and thus should be favorable for detecting high-redshift galaxies. On the other hand, some massive clusters, despite being merging with many subclumps and comprising large Einstein radii, are not necessarily the most prolific in terms of high-redshift galaxies (see for example Acebron et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib2\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib2\">2020</a>), but it is not yet clear if this is a result of cosmic variance, less available area outside the critical curves to search for high-redshift dropouts (see also Oesch et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib45\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib45\">2015</a>), or indication of less a steep faint-end luminosity function than is needed to counter the magnification bias (e.g., Broadhurst et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib13\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib13\">1995</a>; Mashian &amp; Loeb <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib41\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib41\">2013</a>). Ongoing surveys such as the BUFFALO survey (Steinhardt et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib61\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib61\">2020</a>) mapping larger areas around the Hubble Frontier Fields clusters, should be helpful in answering this important question with HST, in the advent of next generation telescopes.</p><p>The largest gravitational lenses known to date (see the list in Table 1 of Acebron et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib2\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib2\">2020</a>) have been usually chosen for HST observations based on their X-ray (MACS clusters and respective snapshot programs, Ebeling et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib22\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib22\">2010</a>; CLASH; Postman et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib51\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib51\">2012</a>) and and SZ signatures (RELICS; Cerny et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib15\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib15\">2018</a>; Paterno-Mahler et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib49\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib49\">2018</a>; Acebron et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib2\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib2\">2020</a>; Coe et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib18\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib18\">2019</a>), or following optical signatures such as giant arcs (e.g., Sharon et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib59\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib59\">2015</a>). In contrast, and although RMJ1212 is \"only\" modestly large\u2014note that it is larger than the typical Hubble Frontier Field cluster\u2014we stress that RMJ1212 was designated as a potentially large lens in a computerized, blind search in ground-based data following only the distribution and luminosity of cluster members as input (Zitrin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib73\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib73\">2012</a>; using the SDSS GMBCG cluster catalog of Hao et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib28\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib28\">2010</a>). Here we confirm that, although somewhat smaller than the preliminary blind estimate of \u223c40'', it is indeed a prominent lens. In Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjabb8ddf1\">1</a> we show the preliminary critical curves predicted by the methodology and mass-to-light scaling of Zitrin et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib73\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib73\">2012</a>). We can compare these curves to the final curves presented here in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjabb8ddf2\">2</a>, derived using the HST data and careful multiple-image identification. Due to the overall successful assumption that light traces mass, the preliminary critical curves, derived from the SDSS data with no multiple images as input, are quite similar in shape to the final curves in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjabb8ddf2\">2</a>, passing in between multiple images as they should and making RMJ1212 another proof-of-concept for identifying the largest lenses directly in ground-based and large sky surveys (Wong et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib67\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib67\">2012</a>; Zitrin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib73\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib73\">2012</a>; Stapelberg et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib60\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib60\">2019</a>). This increasing ability to approximate the projected mass distribution and the corresponding lensing properties can be quite useful for upcoming surveys from the ground, or from space, such as with Euclid or the Nancy Grace Roman Space Telescope (previously known as WFIRST).</p><p>We can also compare the tentative multiple-image identification in our ground-based Gemini data to the final identification presented here. Hubble has the crucial combination of depth and high resolution\u2014a much better angular resolution than in typical ground-based observations (about \u223c0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />1 compared to \u223c1'')\u2014a key for securing the identification of multiple images, especially in lack of spectroscopic redshifts. Nevertheless, two systems and one candidate system that we initially identified in the GMOS data, guided by the location of the preliminary critical curves and following colors and symmetry, survived the more careful inspection allowed by the HST data (at least partially, i.e., some counter images may have been updated). The other two system candidates we marked on the Gemini data seem to be wrong, emphasizing the need for space imaging for extensive multiple-image identification. The HST data allowed us to detect, in addition, several other systems and multiple-image candidates, seen in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjabb8ddf2\">2</a>.</p></div><h3 id=\"apjabb8dds4-2\" name=\"apjabb8dds4-2\">4.2.\u00a0High-redshift Candidates</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>We take advantage of the multiband observations and search the field for high-redshift candidates. Given that the field was observed for only about half an orbit in each WFC3/IR band, the target population are relatively bright objects: the observing scheme was similar to that implemented in the RELICS program, designed to find bright (5<em>\u03c3</em>\u00a0of AB 26.5 in the F160W band, for example) lensed candidates across \u223c40 galaxy clusters.</p><p>First, we search the RELICS-like BPZ catalog for objects with a <em>z</em><sub>best</sub>\u00a0&gt;\u00a05.5. Six objects pass this criterion. Two are designated as likely artifacts close (&lt;1'') to the edge of the WFC3 frame and are discarded. The remaining four objects are listed in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjabb8ddt2\">2</a>, and their stamp images in different bands are shown in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjabb8ddf5\">5</a>. We then take on a second approach. We adopt the Lyman-break technique and apply selection criteria searching explicitly for dropout galaxies. Specifically, we adopt two sets of color criteria for the Lyman-break galaxy selection, as follows:</p><p>The criteria used in Atek et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib4\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib4\">2014</a>; hereafter criteria A):</p><ul class=\"clear-list wd-inline-list\"><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">1.\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\">Redzshift \u223c6\u20137 selection:<ul class=\"clear-list wd-inline-list\"><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">1.1\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\">(F814W\u2013F105W)\u00a0&gt;\u00a01.0</div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">1.2\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\">(F814W\u2013F105W)\u00a0&gt;\u00a0(0.6 + 2.0 \u2217 (F105W\u2013F125W))</div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">1.3\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\">(F105W\u2013F125W)&lt;\u00a00.8</div></li></ul>\n</div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">2.\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\">Redshift \u223c8 selection:<ul class=\"clear-list wd-inline-list\"><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">2.1\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\">(F105W\u2013F125W)\u00a0&gt;\u00a00.5</div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">2.2\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\">(F105W\u2013F125W)\u00a0&gt;\u00a0(0.3 + 1.6 \u2217 (F125W\u2013F140W))</div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">2.3\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\">(F125W-F140W)&lt;\u00a00.5</div></li></ul>And the criteria used in Zheng et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjabb8ddbib72\" id=\"fnref-apjabb8ddbib72\">2014</a>; hereafter criteria B):\n</div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">3.\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\">Redshift \u223c7\u20138 selection:<ul class=\"clear-list wd-inline-list\"><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">3.1\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\">(F814W\u2013F105W)\u00a0&gt;0.8</div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">3.2\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\">(F814W\u2013F105W)\u00a0&gt; (0.8 + (F105W\u2013F125W))</div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">3.3\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\">(F105W\u2013F125W)\u00a0&lt;\u00a00.6</div></li></ul>\n</div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">4.\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\">Redshift \u223c8\u20139 selection:<ul class=\"clear-list wd-inline-list\"><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">4.1\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\">(F105W\u2013F140W)\u00a0&gt;\u00a00.8</div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">4.2\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\">(F105W\u2013F140W)\u00a0&gt;\u00a0(0.8 + (F140W\u2013F160W))</div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">4.3\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\">(F140W\u2013F160W)\u00a0&lt; 0.6</div></li></ul>\n</div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">5.\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\">Redshift \u223c10 selection: (F125W\u2014F160W)\u00a0&gt;\u00a00.8</div></li></ul><p>Here F814W, F105W, F125W, F140W, and F160W refer to the magnitudes in those bands.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjabb8ddf5\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjabb8ddf5\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cfn-live-content-bucket-iop-org.s3.amazonaws.com/journals/0004-637X/903/2/137/1/apjabb8ddf5_lr.jpg?AWSAccessKeyId=AKIAYDKQL6LTV7YY2HIK&amp;Expires=1637000777&amp;Signature=Cd9hO1YCJNpzroXbMC2x0H42g0w%3D\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 5.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden;
